id="content" class="mw-body" role="main">\n\t\t\t<a id="top"></a>\n\t\t\t<div id="siteNotice" class="mw-body-content"><!-- CentralNotice --></div><div class="mw-indicators mw-body-content">\n</div>\n<h1 id="firstHeading" class="firstHeading" lang="en">NCAA Division I</h1>\t\t\t<div id="bodyContent" class="mw-body-content">\n\t\t\t\t<div id="siteSub" class="noprint">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>\t\t\t\t<div id="contentSub"></div>\n\t\t\t\t<div id="jump-to-nav"></div>\t\t\t\t<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>\n\t\t\t\t<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#p-search">Jump to search</a>\n\t\t\t\t<div id="mw-content-text" lang="en" dir="ltr" class="mw-content-ltr"><div class="mw-parser-output"><p class="mw-empty-elt">\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a href="/wiki/File:NCAA_logo.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/NCAA_logo.svg/200px-NCAA_logo.svg.png" width="200" height="197" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/NCAA_logo.svg/300px-NCAA_logo.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/NCAA_logo.svg/400px-NCAA_logo.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="117" data-file-height="115" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:NCAA_logo.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Main logo used by the NCAA in Divisions I, II, and III.</div></div></div>\n<p><b>NCAA Division I</b> (<b>D-I</b>) is the highest level of intercollegiate athletics sanctioned by the <a href="/wiki/National_Collegiate_Athletic_Association" title="National Collegiate Athletic Association">National Collegiate Athletic Association</a> (NCAA) in the <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a>. D-I schools include the major collegiate athletic powers, with larger budgets, more elaborate facilities and more <a href="/wiki/Athletic_scholarship" title="Athletic scholarship">athletic scholarships</a> than Divisions II and III as well as many smaller schools committed to the highest level of intercollegiate competition.\n</p><p>This level was once called the University Division of the NCAA, in contrast to the lower level College Division; these terms were replaced with numeric divisions in 1973. The University Division was renamed Division I, while the College Division was split in two; the College Division members that offered scholarships or wanted to compete against those who did became <a href="/wiki/NCAA_Division_II" title="NCAA Division II">Division II</a>, while those who did not want to offer scholarships became <a href="/wiki/NCAA_Division_III" title="NCAA Division III">Division III</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>For the 2014-15 school year, Division I contained 345 of the NCAA\'s 1,066 member institutions, with 125 in FBS, 125 in FCS, and 95 non-football schools, with six additional schools in the transition from Division II to Division I.<sup id="cite_ref-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> There was a moratorium on any additional movement up to D-I until 2012, after which any school that wants to move to D-I must be accepted for membership by a conference and show the NCAA it has the financial ability to support a D-I program.\n</p>\n<div id="toc" class="toc"><input type="checkbox" role="button" id="toctogglecheckbox" class="toctogglecheckbox" style="display:none" /><div class="toctitle" lang="en" dir="ltr"><h2>Contents</h2><span class="toctogglespan"><label class="toctogglelabel" for="toctogglecheckbox"></label></span></div>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-1"><a href="#D-I_schools"><span class="tocnumber">1</span> FCS</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-3"><a href="#Finances"><span class="tocnumber">2</span> <span class="toctext">Finances</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 sport</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-12"><a href="#Rules_for_multi-sport_athletes"><span class="tocnumber">6.1</span> <span class="toctext">Rules for multi-sport athletes</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-13"><a href="#Football_subdivisions"><span class="tocnumber">7</span> <span class="toctext">Football subdivisions</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-14"><a href="#Football_Bowl_Subdivision"><span class="tocnumber">7.1</span> <span class="toctext">Football Bowl Subdivision</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-15"><a href="#Conferences_2"><span class="tocnumber">7.2</span> <span class="toctext">Conferences</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-16"><a href="#Football_Championship_Subdivision"><span class="tocnumber">7.3</span> <span class="toctext">Football Championship Subdivision</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-17"><a href="#Abstainers"><span class="tocnumber">7.4</span> <span class="toctext">Abstainers</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-18"><a href="#Scholarships"><span class="tocnumber">7.5</span> <span class="toctext">Scholarships</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-19"><a href="#Conferences_3"><span class="tocnumber">7.6</span> <span class="toctext">Conferences</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-20"><a href="#Division_I_non-football_schools"><span class="tocnumber">7.7</span> <span class="toctext">Division I class="tocnumber">12</span> <span class="toctext">External links</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</div>\n\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="D-I_schools">D-I schools</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=NCAA_Division_I&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: D-I schools">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>Schools must field teams in at least seven sports for men and seven for women or six for men and eight for women, with at least two team sports for each gender.<sup id="cite_ref-Sponsorship_requirements_4-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sponsorship_requirements-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-NCAA_Division_Differences_5-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NCAA_Division_Differences-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> Teams that include both men and women are counted as men\'s sports for the purposes of sponsorship counting.<sup id="cite_ref-Sponsorship_requirements_4-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sponsorship_requirements-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> Division I schools must meet minimum financial aid awards for their athletics program, and there are maximum financial aid awards for each sport that a Division I school cannot exceed.<sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> Several other NCAA sanctioned minimums and differences distinguish Division I from Divisions II and III.<sup id="cite_ref-NCAA_Division_Differences_5-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NCAA_Division_Differences-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> Members must sponsor at least one team sport for each sex in each playing season (fall, winter, spring), again with coeducational teams counted as men\'s teams for this purpose.<sup id="cite_ref-Team_sport_requirements_7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Team_sport_requirements-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> There are contest and participant minimums for each sport, as well as scheduling criteria. For sports other than football and basketball, Division I schools must play 100 percent of the minimum number of contests against Division I opponents\xe2\x80\x94anything over the minimum number of games has to be 50 percent Division I. Men\'s and women\'s basketball teams have to play all but two games against Division I teams; for men, they must play one-third of all their contests in the home <a href="/wiki/List_of_NCAA_Division_I_basketball_arenas" title="List of NCAA Division I basketball arenas">arena</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In addition to the schools that compete fully as D-I institutions, the NCAA allows D-II and D-III schools to classify one men\'s and one women\'s sport (other than football or basketball) as a D-I sport, as long as they sponsored those sports before the latest rules change in 2011.<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> Also, Division II schools are eligible to compete for Division I national championships in sports that do not have a Division II national championship, and in those sports may also operate under D-I rules and scholarship limits.<sup id="cite_ref-No_D-II_championship_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-No_D-II_championship-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="FBS_and_FCS">FBS and FCS</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=NCAA_Division_I&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: FBS and FCS">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>For <a href="/wiki/College_football" title="College football">football</a> only, Division I was further subdivided in 1978 into <b>Division I-A</b> (the principal football schools), <b>Division I-AA</b> (the other schools with football teams), and <b>Division I</b> (those schools not sponsoring football).<sup id="cite_ref-bswb_11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bswb-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> In 2006, Division I-A and I-AA were renamed "<b><a href="/wiki/NCAA_Division_I_Football_Bowl_Subdivision" title="NCAA Division I Football Bowl Subdivision">Football Bowl Subdivision</a></b>" (FBS) and "<b>Football Championship Subdivision</b>" (FCS), respectively.\n</p><p>FBS teams are allowed a maximum of 85 players receiving athletically based aid per year, with each player allowed to receive up to a full scholarship. FCS teams have the same 85-player limit as FBS teams, but are only allowed to give aid equivalent to 63 full scholarships. FCS teams are allowed to award partial scholarships, a practice technically allowed but essentially never used at the FBS level.\n</p><p>FBS teams also have to meet minimum game attendance requirements (average 15,000 people in actual or paid attendance per home game), while FCS teams do not need to meet minimum attendance requirements.\n</p><p>Another difference is postseason play. Since 1978, FCS teams have played in the <a href="/wiki/NCAA_Division_I_Football_Championship" title="NCAA Division I Football Championship">NCAA Division I Football Championship</a>, a playoff tournament to determine a NCAA-sanctioned national champion. Meanwhile, the FBS teams play in <a href="/wiki/Bowl_game" title="Bowl game">bowl games</a>, where various polls rank the number one team after the conclusion of the bowl games. Starting with the 2014 postseason, a four-team playoff called the <a href="/wiki/College_Football_Playoff" title="College Football Playoff">College Football Playoff</a> replaced the previous one-game championship format. Even so, Division I FBS football is still the only NCAA sport in which a yearly champion is not determined by an NCAA-sanctioned championship event.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Finances">Finances</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=NCAA_Division_I&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Finances">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>Division I athletic programs generated $8.7 billion in revenue in the 2009\xe2\x80\x932010 academic year. Men\'s teams provided 55%, women\'s teams 15%, and 30% was not categorized by sex or sport. Football and men\'s basketball are usually a university\'s only profitable sports,<sup id="cite_ref-thomas20110426_13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-thomas20110426-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> and are called "revenue sports".<sup id="cite_ref-mandel20140623_14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mandel20140623-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup> From 2008 to 2012, 205 varsity teams were dropped in NCAA Division I &#8211; 72 for women and 133 for men, with men\'s tennis, gymnastics and wrestling hit particularly hard.<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In the Football Bowl Subdivision (130 schools in 2017), between 50 and 60 percent of football and men\'s basketball programs generated positive revenues (above program expenses).<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> However, in the Football Championship Subdivision (124 schools in 2017), only four percent of football and five percent of men\'s basketball programs generated positive revenues.<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 2012, 2% of athletic budgets were spent on equipment, uniforms and supplies for male athletes at NCAA Division I Football Bowl Subdivision school, with the median spending per-school at $742,000.<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 2014, the NCAA and the student athletes debated whether student athletes should be paid. In April, the NCAA approved students-athletes getting free unlimited meals and snacks. The NCAA stated "The adoption of the meals legislation finished a conversation that began in the Awards, Benefits, Expenses and Financial Aid Cabinet. Members have worked to find appropriate ways to ensure student-athletes get the nutrition they need without jeopardizing Pell Grants or other federal aid received by the neediest student-athletes. With their vote, members of the council said they believe loosening NCAA rules on what and when food can be provided from athletics departments is the best way to address the issue." \n<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Conferences">Conferences</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=NCAA_Division_I&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Conferences">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>Under NCAA regulations, all Division I conferences defined as "multisport conferences" must meet the following criteria:<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<ul><li>A total of at least 7 active Division I members.</li>\n<li>Separate from the above, at least 7 active Division I members that sponsor both men\'s and women\'s basketball.</li>\n<li>Sponsorship of at least 12 NCAA Division I sports.</li>\n<li>Minimum of 6 men\'s sports, with the following additional restrictions:\n<ul><li>Men\'s basketball is a mandatory sport, and at least 7 members must sponsor that sport.</li>\n<li>Non-football conferences must sponsor at least two men\'s team sports other than basketball.</li>\n<li>At least 6 members must sponsor five men\'s sports other than basketball, including either football or two other team sports.</li></ul></li>\n<li>Minimum of 6 women\'s sports, with the following additional restrictions:\n<ul><li>Women\'s basketball is a mandatory sport, with at least 7 members sponsoring that sport.</li>\n<li>At least two other women\'s team sports must be sponsored.</li>\n<li>At least 6 members must sponsor five women\'s sports other than basketball, including either football or two other team sports. If a conference officially sponsors an NCAA "emerging sport" for women (currently equestrianism, rugby union, or triathlon), that sport will be counted if 5 members (instead of 6) sponsor it.</li></ul></li></ul>\n<p>FBS Conferences\n</p><p>FBS conferences must meet a more stringent set of requirements for NCAA recognition than other conferences:<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<ul><li>A total of at least 8 active FBS members.</li>\n<li>To be counted toward this total, a school must participate in conference play in at least 6 men\'s and 8 women\'s sports, including men\'s and women\'s basketball, football, and at least two other women\'s team sports.\n<ul><li>Each school may count one men\'s and one women\'s sport not sponsored by its primary conference toward the above limits, as long as that sport competes in another Division I conference. The men\'s and women\'s sports so counted need not be the same sport.</li></ul></li></ul>\n<table class="wikitable sortable">\n\n<tbody><tr>\n<th>Conference\n</th>\n<th>Nickname\n</th>\n<th>Founded\n</th>\n<th>Members\n</th>\n<th>Sports\n</th>\n<th>Headquarters\n</th>\n<th>Total<br />NCAA<br Football Playoff">College Football Playoff</a>)\n</p>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_NCAA_Division_I_FBS_football_programs" title="List of NCAA Division I FBS football programs">List of NCAA Division I FBS football programs</a></div>\n<dl><dt>Notes</dt></dl>\n<div class="reflist" style="list-style-type: lower-alpha;">\n<div class="mw-references-wrap mw-references-columns"><ol class="references">\n<li id="cite_note-22"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-22">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The conference was founded in 1979 as the <a href="/wiki/Big_East_Conference_(1979%E2%80%932013)" title="Big East Conference (1979\xe2\x80\x932013)">original Big East Conference</a>. It renamed itself the American Athletic Conference following a <a href="/wiki/2010%E2%80%9313_Big_East_Conference_realignment" title="2010\xe2\x80\x9313 Big East Conference realignment">2013 split</a> along football lines. The non-FBS schools of the original conference left to form a new conference that purchased <a href="/wiki/Big_East_Conference" title="Big East Conference">the Big East name</a>, while the FBS schools continued to operate under the old Big East\'s charter and structure. The American also inherited the old Big East\'s <a href="/wiki/Bowl_Championship_Series" title="Bowl Championship Series">Bowl Championship Series</a> berth for the 2013 season, the last for the BCS.</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-23"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-23">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">11 of the 12 full members sponsor football, with <a href="/wiki/Wichita_State_University" title="Wichita State University">Wichita State</a> as the only non-football member.</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-24"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-24">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">In addition to the full members, six schools have single-sport associate membership:\n<ul><li><a href="/wiki/United_States_Naval_Academy" title="United States Naval Academy">Navy</a> is a football-only member.</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/University_of_Florida" title="University of Florida">Florida</a> and <a href="/wiki/Vanderbilt_University" title="Vanderbilt University">Vanderbilt</a> are members in women\'s lacrosse.</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Old_Dominion_University" title="Old Dominion University">Old Dominion</a>, <a href="/wiki/California_State_University,_Sacramento" title="California State University, Sacramento">Sacramento State</a> and <a href="/wiki/San_Diego_State_University" title="San Diego State University">San Diego State</a> are members in women\'s rowing.</li></ul>\n</span></li>\n<li id="cite_note-25"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-25">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="/wiki/University_of_Notre_Dame" title="University of Notre Dame">Notre Dame</a> is a full member except in football, in which it remains independent. It has committed to play five games each season against ACC opponents, and to play each other ACC member at least once every three years.</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-26"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-26">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">In addition to the full members, two schools have affiliate membership:\n<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Johns_Hopkins_University" title="Johns Hopkins University">Johns Hopkins</a>, otherwise a <a href="/wiki/NCAA_Division_III" title="NCAA Division III">Division III</a> member, is an affiliate in both <a href="/wiki/Johns_Hopkins_Blue_Jays_men%27s_lacrosse" title="Johns Hopkins Blue Jays men&#39;s lacrosse">men\'s</a> and <a href="/wiki/Johns_Hopkins_Blue_Jays_women%27s_lacrosse" title="Johns Hopkins Blue Jays women&#39;s lacrosse">women\'s</a> lacrosse, sports in which the school fields Division I teams.</li>\n<li>Notre Dame is a men\'s hockey affiliate.</li></ul>\n</span></li>\n<li id="cite_note-27"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-27">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">In addition to the full members, the Big 12 has 11 members that participate in only one sport:\n<ul><li><a href="/wiki/United_States_Air_Force_Academy" title="United States Air Force Academy">Air Force</a>, <a href="/wiki/California_State_University,_Fresno" title="California State University, Fresno">Fresno State</a>, <a href="/wiki/North_Dakota_State_University" title="North Dakota State University">North Dakota State</a>, <a href="/wiki/University_of_Northern_Colorado" title="University of Northern Colorado">Northern Colorado</a>, <a href="/wiki/University_of_Northern_Iowa" title="University of Northern Iowa">Northern Iowa</a>, <a href="/wiki/South_Dakota_State_University" title="South Dakota State University">South Dakota State</a>, <a href="/wiki/Utah_Valley_University" title="Utah Valley University">Utah Valley</a>, and <a href="/wiki/University_of_Wyoming" title="University of Wyoming">Wyoming</a> in wrestling.</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/University_of_Alabama" title="University of Alabama">Alabama</a> and <a href="/wiki/University_of_Tennessee" title="University of Tennessee">Tennessee</a> in women\'s rowing.</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/University_of_Denver" title="University of Denver">Denver</a> in women\'s gymnastics.</li></ul>\n</span></li>\n<li id="cite_note-28"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-28">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The conference was founded in 1995, with football competition starting in 1996.</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-29"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-29">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">In addition to the 14 full members, Conference USA features three schools that play <a href="/wiki/College_soccer" title="College soccer">men\'s soccer</a> in the conference: <a href="/wiki/University_of_Kentucky" title="University of Kentucky">Kentucky</a>, <a href="/wiki/University_of_New_Mexico" title="University of New Mexico">New Mexico</a>, and <a href="/wiki/University_of_South_Carolina" title="University of South Carolina">South Carolina</a>. New Mexico will drop men\'s soccer after the 2018 season.</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-30"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-30">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Note that "Independents" is not a conference; it is simply a designation used for schools whose football programs do not play in any conference. All of these schools have conference memberships for other sports.</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-31"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-31">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">In addition to the 12 full members, the Mid-American Conference features nine members which only participate in one sport each, plus one other school that competes in two sports.\n<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Appalachian_State_University" title="Appalachian State University">Appalachian State</a> and <a href="/wiki/Longwood_University" title="Longwood University">Longwood</a> compete in <a href="/wiki/Field_hockey" title="Field hockey">women\'s field hockey</a>.</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Binghamton_University" title="Binghamton University">Binghamton</a> competes in men\'s tennis.</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/University_of_Evansville" title="University of Evansville">Evansville</a>, <a href="/wiki/Missouri_State_University" title="Missouri State University">Missouri State</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Southern_Illinois_University_Carbondale" title="Southern Illinois University Carbondale">Southern Illinois</a> compete in men\'s swimming and diving.</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/University_of_Missouri" title="University of Missouri">Missouri</a> and <a href="/wiki/Old_Dominion_University" title="Old Dominion University">Old Dominion</a> compete in <a href="/wiki/Collegiate_wrestling" title="Collegiate wrestling">wrestling</a>.</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/West_Virginia_University" title="West Virginia University">West Virginia</a> competes in men\'s soccer.</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Southern_Illinois_University_Edwardsville" title="Southern Illinois University Edwardsville">SIU Edwardsville</a> competes in men\'s soccer and wrestling.</li></ul>\n</span></li>\n<li id="cite_note-32"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-32">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Since 2012, <a href="/wiki/University_of_Hawaii_at_Manoa" title="University of Hawaii at Manoa">Hawai\xca\xbbi</a> has been a football-only associate member, with most of its remaining teams in the non-football <a href="/wiki/Big_West_Conference" title="Big West Conference">Big West Conference</a>.</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-33"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-33">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">In addition to the 11 full members and football affiliate Hawai\xca\xbbi, <a href="/wiki/Colorado_College" title="Colorado College">Colorado College</a>, a Division III school with a Division I <a href="/wiki/Colorado_College_Tigers_men%27s_ice_hockey" title="Colorado College Tigers men&#39;s ice hockey">men\'s ice hockey team</a>, plays Division I women\'s soccer in the MW.</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-34"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-34">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The charter of the Pac-12 dates only to the formation of the Athletic Association of Western Universities (AAWU) in 1959. However, the Pac-12 claims the history of the <a href="/wiki/Pacific_Coast_Conference" title="Pacific Coast Conference">Pacific Coast Conference</a>, which was founded in 1915 and began competition in 1916, as its own. Of the nine members of the PCC at the time of its demise in June 1959, only <a href="/wiki/University_of_Idaho" title="University of Idaho">Idaho</a> never joined the Pac-12. The PCC\'s berth in the <a href="/wiki/Rose_Bowl_Game" title="Rose Bowl Game">Rose Bowl</a> passed to the AAWU.</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-35"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-35">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The Pac-12 also includes several associate members which compete in one or two sports in the conference. <a href="/wiki/San_Diego_State_University" title="San Diego State University">San Diego State</a> plays men\'s soccer. <a href="/wiki/California_State_University,_Bakersfield" title="California State University, Bakersfield">Cal State Bakersfield</a> and <a href="/wiki/California_Polytechnic_State_University" title="California Polytechnic State University">Cal Poly</a> compete in <a href="/wiki/Collegiate_wrestling" title="Collegiate wrestling">wrestling</a>. Cal Poly also participates in men\'s <a href="/wiki/Swimming_(sport)" title="Swimming (sport)">swimming</a> and <a href="/wiki/Diving" title="Diving">diving</a>, which the NCAA considers a single sport. <a href="/wiki/University_of_California,_Santa_Barbara" title="University of California, Santa Barbara">UC Santa Barbara</a> only competes in men\'s swimming and diving.</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-36"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-36">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Ten Sun Belt Conference members currently sponsor football, with <a href="/wiki/University_of_Arkansas_at_Little_Rock" title="University of Arkansas at Little Rock">Little Rock</a> and <a href="/wiki/University_of_Texas_at_Arlington" title="University of Texas at Arlington">UT Arlington</a> as members that do not play football at all.</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-37"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-37">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="/wiki/Howard_University" title="Howard University">Howard</a> is a men\'s soccer affiliate. <a href="/wiki/University_of_Central_Arkansas" title="University of Central Arkansas">Central Arkansas</a> will join in men\'s soccer in 2019.</span>\n</li>\n</ol></div></div>\n<p>FCS Conferences\n</p>\n<table class="wikitable allow its champion to participate in the <a href="/wiki/Celebration_Bowl" title="Celebration Bowl">Celebration Bowl</a>. Non-champions are eligible for at-large bids (an example being the <a href="/wiki/2016_North_Carolina_A%26T_Aggies_football_team" title="2016 North Carolina A&amp;T Aggies football team">2016 North Carolina A&amp;T Aggies football team</a>).\n</p><p>% The SWAC abstains from the championship tournament to allow for a longer regular season, an in-conference championship game (until 2017), and (since 2015) participation in the <a href="/wiki/Celebration_Bowl" title="Celebration Bowl">Celebration Bowl</a>. Beginning 2018, the SWAC will discontinue its championship game and send its regular season champion to the Celebration Bowl, freeing up the conference\'s second-place finisher (if it is not Grambling State, Alabama State or Southern) to accept an at-large bid.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Sports">Sports</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a title="NCAA Men&#39;s Water Polo Championship">California (13)</a>\n</td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p><small>Sports are ranked according to total possible scholarships (number of teams x number of scholarships per team). Scholarship numbers for head-count sports are indicated without a decimal point. Numbers for equivalency sports are indicated with a decimal point, with a trailing zero if needed.</small>\n</p><p>Notes:\n</p>\n<ul><li>Football \xe2\x80\x94 D-I football programs are divided into FBS and FCS. The 128 FBS programs can award financial aid to as many as 85 players, with each player able to receive up to a full scholarship. The 124 FCS programs can award up to the equivalent of 63 full scholarships, divided among no more than 85 individuals. Some FCS conferences restrict scholarships to a lower level or prohibit scholarships altogether.</li>\n<li>Soccer \xe2\x80\x94 The Big 12 and the SEC are the only two major traditional D-I conferences that do not sponsor soccer. Several other D-I conferences also do not sponsor the sport\xe2\x80\x94the Big Sky, MEAC, Mountain West, Ohio Valley, Southland, and SWAC.</li>\n<li>Ice Hockey \xe2\x80\x94 Almost all D-I ice hockey programs are in the <a href="/wiki/Northeastern_United_States" title="Northeastern United States">Northeast</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Upper_Midwest" title="Upper Midwest">Upper Midwest</a>, or the <a href="/wiki/Front_Range_Urban_Corridor" title="Front Range Urban Corridor">Colorado Front Range</a>. Only one D-I all-sports conference, the <a href="/wiki/Big_Ten_Conference" title="Big Ten Conference">Big Ten</a>, sponsors a men\'s hockey league. All other conferences operate as hockey-specific leagues. Of the 59 D-I hockey schools, 22 are otherwise classified as either D-II or D-III; a number of schools from D-II play in D-I in Ice Hockey as the NCAA no longer sponsors a championship in D-II and many have traditional/cultural fan bases that support ice hockey, and the D-III schools were "grandfathered" in to D-I through their having sponsored hockey prior to the creation of D-III.</li>\n<li>Lacrosse \xe2\x80\x94 The vast majority of D-I lacrosse programs are from the Northeast and <a href="/wiki/Mid-Atlantic_states" class="mw-redirect" title="Mid-Atlantic states">Mid-Atlantic</a>. There are only two D-I programs west of the Mississippi, both on the Colorado Front Range (<a href="/wiki/Air_Force_Falcons" title="Air Force Falcons">Air Force</a> and <a href="/wiki/Denver_Pioneers_men%27s_lacrosse" title="Denver Pioneers men&#39;s lacrosse">Denver</a>). Utah is set to become the third D-I program west of the Mississippi in the 2019 season (2018\xe2\x80\x9319 school year)</li>\n<li>Volleyball \xe2\x80\x94 Of the traditional D-I conferences, only the <a href="/wiki/Big_West_Conference" title="Big West Conference">Big West</a> sponsors men\'s volleyball, and it did not do so until the 2017\xe2\x80\x9318 school year. Two of the other three major volleyball conferences, defined here as leagues that include full Division I members, are volleyball-specific conferences; the third is the <a href="/wiki/Mountain_Pacific_Sports_Federation" title="Mountain Pacific Sports Federation">Mountain Pacific Sports Federation</a>, a multi-sport conference that does not sponsor football or basketball. In addition to the D-I schools, 23 D-II schools compete in D-I volleyball (with two more set to add the sport for 2018\xe2\x80\x9319); nine of these are members of <a href="/wiki/Conference_Carolinas" title="Conference Carolinas">Conference Carolinas</a>, the only all-sports league outside Division III to sponsor the sport.</li>\n<li>Water Polo \xe2\x80\x94 The number of D-I schools sponsoring men\'s water polo has declined from 35 in 1987/88 to 22 in 2010/11.<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> No school outside of California has ever made the finals of the championship, and all champions since 1998 have come from one of the four California based Pac-12 schools.</li></ul>\n<h3><span id="Men.27s_individual_sports"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Men\'s_individual_sports">Men\'s individual sports</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=NCAA_Division_I&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Men&#039;s individual sports">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The following table lists the men\'s individual DI sports with at least 1,000 participating athletes. Numbers for head-count sports are indicated without a decimal point; those for equivalency sports are indicated with a decimal point, with a trailing zero if needed.</li>\n<li>Women\'s soccer is the fastest growing NCAA D-I women\'s team sport over a prolonged period, increasing from 22 teams in 1981/82 to 315 teams in 2010/11.<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup> However, in recent years, the fastest-growing has been beach volleyball, which went from 14 Division I teams in 2011\xe2\x80\x9312 to 55 in 2016\xe2\x80\x9317.</li>\n<li><sup>\xe2\x80\xa0</sup> = In the 2016\xe2\x80\x9317 school year, rugby is classified by the NCAA as an "emerging sport" for women. Beach volleyball, which had previously been an "emerging sport" under the name of "sand volleyball",<sup id="cite_ref-Beach_volleyball_45-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Beach_volleyball-45">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup> became an official NCAA championship sport in 2015\xe2\x80\x9316.<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup></li>\n<li>* = The number of scholarships are partially linked for (indoor) volleyball and beach volleyball. Schools that field both indoor and beach volleyball teams are allowed 6.0 full scholarship equivalents specifically for beach volleyball as of 2016\xe2\x80\x9317, with the further limitations that (1) no player receiving aid for beach volleyball can be on the indoor volleyball roster and (2) a maximum of 14 individuals can receive aid in beach volleyball. If a school fields only a beach volleyball team, it is allowed 8.0 full scholarship equivalents for that sport, also distributed among no more than 14 individuals.</li></ul>\n<h3><span id="Women.27s_individual_sports"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Women\'s_individual_sports">Women\'s individual sports</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=NCAA_Division_I&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Women&#039;s individual sports">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The following table lists the women\'s individual DI sports with at least 1,000 participating athletes. Sports are ranked by number of athletes.\n</p>\n<table class="wikitable sortable" style="text-align: center;">\n\n<tbody><tr>\n<th width="40">No.\n</th>\n<th width="145">Sport\n</th>\n<th width="75">Teams (2015)<sup id="cite_ref-ncaapublications.com_41-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ncaapublications.com-41">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup>\n</th>\n<th width="75">Teams (1982)<sup id="cite_ref-ncaapublications.com_41-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ncaapublications.com-41">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup>\n</th>\n<th width="65">Change\n</th>\n<th width="90">Athletes<sup id="cite_ref-ncaapublications.com_41-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ncaapublications.com-41">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup>\n</th>\n<th width="95">Season\n</th></tr>\n<tr>\n<th>1\n</th>\n<td>Track (outdoor)\n</td>\n<td>329\n</td>\n<td>180\n</td>\n<td>+149\n</td>\n<td>13,075\n</td>\n<td>Spring\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<th>2\n</th>\n<td>Track (indoor)\n</td>\n<td>319\n</td>\n<td>127\n</td>\n<td>+192\n</td>\n<td>12,816\n</td>\n<td>Winter\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<th>3\n</th>\n<td>Cross country\n</td>\n<td>342\n</td>\n<td>183\n</td>\n<td>+159\n</td>\n<td>6,031\n</td>\n<td>Fall\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<th>4\n</th>\n<td>Swimming &amp; These contracts can be quite lucrative, particularly for DI schools from the biggest conferences. For example, the Big Ten conference in 2016 entered into contracts with Fox and ESPN that pay the conference $2.64 billion over six years.\n</p><p>The NCAA also holds certain TV contracts. For example, the NCAA\xe2\x80\x99s contract to show the men\xe2\x80\x99s basketball championship tournament (widely known as March Madness) is currently under a 14-year deal with CBS and Turner that runs from 2010 to 2024 and pays $11 billion.\n</p><p>For the 2014\xe2\x80\x9315 fiscal year, the conferences that earned the most revenues (and that distributed the most revenues to each of their member schools) were: \n</p>\n<ol><li>SEC \xe2\x80\x94 $527 million (dispersed $33 million to each of its member schools)</li>\n<li>Big 10 \xe2\x80\x94 $449 million (dispersed $32 million each)</li>\n<li>Pac 12 \xe2\x80\x94 $439 million (dispersed $25 million each)</li>\n<li>ACC \xe2\x80\x94 $403 million (dispersed $26 million each)</li>\n<li>Big 12 \xe2\x80\x94 $268 million (dispersed $23 million each)</li></ol>\n<table class="wikitable sortable" style="text-align:center">\n\n<caption>U.S. college sports sports TV rights\n</caption>\n<tbody><tr>\n<th>Sports America Conference">Mid America Conference</a> (MAC)</td>\n<td>All</td>\n<td>ESPN</td>\n<td>$100m ($8m)</td>\n<td><sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup>\n</td></tr></tbody></table>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Scholarship_limits_by_sport">Scholarship limits by sport</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=NCAA_Division_I&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Scholarship limits by sport">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>The NCAA has limits on the total financial aid each Division I member may award in each sport that the school sponsors. It divides sports that are sponsored into two types for purposes of scholarship limitations:\n</p>\n<ul><li>"Head-count" sports, in which the NCAA limits the total number of individuals that can receive athletic scholarships, but allows each player to receive up to a full scholarship.</li>\n<li>"Equivalency" sports, in which the NCAA limits the total financial aid that a school can offer in a given sport to the equivalent of a set number of full scholarships. Roster limitations may or may not apply, depending on the sport.</li></ul>\n<p>The term "counter" is also key to this concept. The NCAA defines a "counter" as "an individual who is receiving institutional financial aid that is countable against the aid limitations in a sport."<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The number of scholarships that Division I members may award in each sport is listed below. In this table, scholarship numbers for head-count sports are indicated <i>without a decimal point</i>; for equivalency sports, they are listed with a decimal point, with a trailing zero if required.\n</p>\n<table class="wikitable sortable" style="">\n\n<tbody><tr>\n<th>Sport</th>\n<th>Men\'s</th>\n<th>Women\'s\n</th></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><a href="/wiki/College_baseball" title="College class="reflist" style="list-style-type: decimal;">\n<div class="mw-references-wrap"><ol class="references">\n<li id="cite_note-57"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-57">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">This total is also subject to the following restrictions:\n<ul><li>The number of total counters is limited to 27.<sup id="cite_ref-Baseball1_51-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Baseball1-51">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup></li>\n<li>Each counter must receive "athletically related and other countable financial aid" equal to at least 25% of a full scholarship.<sup id="cite_ref-Baseball2_52-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Baseball2-52">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup> Most institutional and governmental non-athletic aid falls in the "countable" category;<sup id="cite_ref-Countable_53-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Countable-53">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup> an official NCAA rules interpretation also allows schools to count aid that would otherwise be exempt by NCAA rule (such as purely academic awards) toward the 25% limit, as long as it also is included in the calculations for the team equivalency limit.<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup>  The 25% rule does not apply to baseball schools that offer only need-based aid (such as <a href="/wiki/Ivy_League" title="Ivy League">Ivy League</a> members).<sup id="cite_ref-Baseball3_55-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Baseball3-55">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup> A second exception to the 25% rule, added in 2012, is for players in their final year of athletic eligibility who have not previously received athletically related aid in baseball at any college.<sup id="cite_ref-Baseball4_56-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Baseball4-56">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup></li></ul>\n</span></li>\n<li id="cite_note-62"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-62">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">This total is for schools that also sponsor women\'s indoor volleyball.<sup id="cite_ref-BeachPlusIndoorVB_60-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BeachPlusIndoorVB-60">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup> If a school does not sponsor women\'s indoor volleyball, it is allowed 8.0 equivalents for beach volleyball.<sup id="cite_ref-BeachNoIndoorVB_61-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BeachNoIndoorVB-61">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup> For all schools, the maximum number of counters in beach volleyball is 14.<sup id="cite_ref-BeachPlusIndoorVB_60-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BeachPlusIndoorVB-60">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-BeachNoIndoorVB_61-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BeachNoIndoorVB-61">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup></span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-66"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-66">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">If a school sponsors men\'s cross-country but does not sponsor either indoor or outdoor track and field for men, it is allowed 5.0 scholarship equivalents for that sport.<sup id="cite_ref-XC,_no_TF_65-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-XC,_no_TF-65">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup></span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-67"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-67">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">If a school sponsors women\'s cross-country but does not sponsor either indoor or outdoor track and field for women, it is allowed 6.0 scholarship equivalents for that sport.<sup id="cite_ref-XC,_no_TF_65-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-XC,_no_TF-65">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup></span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-69"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-69">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">FBS programs are also limited to 25 new counters per school year.<sup id="cite_ref-FBS_scholarship_limits_68-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FBS_scholarship_limits-68">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup></span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-72"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-72">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">FCS programs are also limited to 85 total counters per school year.<sup id="cite_ref-FCS_scholarship_limits_70-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FCS_scholarship_limits-70">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup> Effective with the recruiting cycle for the 2018\xe2\x80\x9319 school year, the previous limit of 30 new counters per year for FCS programs has been removed.<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup></span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-75"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-75">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The number of total counters is limited to 30.<sup id="cite_ref-IceHockeyM_74-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IceHockeyM-74">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup></span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-77"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-77">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The NCAA Division I Manual does not include any scholarship limitations for women\'s ice hockey. These limitations are instead found in the Division II Manual.<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup> Note also that the Division II Manual does not include any limit on total counters for any sport, including women\'s ice hockey.</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-78"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-78">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The NCAA classifies rifle as a men\'s sport, despite the fact that competitions are fully coeducational. Of the 33 NCAA <a href="/wiki/List_of_NCAA_rifle_programs" title="List of NCAA rifle programs">rifle schools</a> (23 in Division I, 4 in Division II, and 6 in Division III), 22 field a single coed/mixed team. Six schools (five in Division I and one in Division III) field women-only teams. Schools are also allowed to field any combination of men\'s, women\'s, and mixed teams. Currently, one D-I school and one D-III school field separate men\'s and women\'s teams; one D-I school and one D-II school field a women-only team plus a mixed team; and one D-I school (<a href="/wiki/VMI_Keydets" title="VMI Keydets">VMI</a>) fields all three types of teams. The scholarship limits are per school, not per team.</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-TriathlonW-79"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-TriathlonW_79-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The NCAA adopted triathlon as its newest "emerging sport" for women effective with the 2014\xe2\x80\x9315 school year, with an initial limit of 3.5 scholarship equivalents. The total number of equivalents reached its final value of 6.5 in 2017\xe2\x80\x9318.<sup id="cite_ref-EquivalencyW_63-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EquivalencyW-63">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup></span>\n</li>\n</ol></div></div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Rules_for_multi-sport_athletes">Rules for multi-sport athletes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=NCAA_Division_I&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Rules for multi-sport athletes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The NCAA also has rules specifying the sport in which multi-sport athletes are to be counted, with the basic rules being:<sup id="cite_ref-Multisport_80-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Multisport-80">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<ul><li>Anyone who participates in football is counted in that sport, even if he does not receive financial aid from the football program. An exception exists for players at non-scholarship FCS programs who receive aid in another sport.<sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup></li>\n<li>Participants in basketball are counted in that sport, unless they also play football.</li>\n<li>Participants in men\'s ice hockey are counted in that sport, unless they also play football or basketball.</li>\n<li>Participants in both men\'s swimming and diving and men\'s water polo are counted in swimming and diving, unless they count in football or basketball.</li>\n<li>Participants in women\'s (indoor) volleyball are counted in that sport unless they also play basketball.</li>\n<li>All other multi-sport athletes are counted in whichever sport the school chooses.</li></ul>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Football_subdivisions">Football subdivisions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=NCAA_Division_I&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Football subdivisions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>Subdivisions in Division I exist only in <a href="/wiki/American_football" title="American football">football</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-usatoday.com_82-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-usatoday.com-82">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup> In all other sports, all Division I conferences are equivalent. The subdivisions were recently given names to reflect the differing levels of football play in them.\n</p><p>The method by which the NCAA determines whether a school is Bowl or Championship subdivision is first by attendance numbers and then by scholarships.<sup id="cite_ref-Bowl_Subdivision_Requirements_84-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bowl_Subdivision_Requirements-84">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup> For attendance reporting methods, the NCAA allows schools to report either total tickets sold or the number of persons in attendance at the games. They require a minimum average of 15,000 people in attendance every other year.<sup id="cite_ref-Bowl_Subdivision_Requirements_84-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bowl_Subdivision_Requirements-84">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup> These numbers get posted to the NCAA statistics website for football each year. With the new rules starting in the 2006 season, the number of Bowl Subdivision schools could drop in the future if those schools are not able to pull in enough fans into the games. Additionally, 14 FCS schools had enough attendance to be moved up in 2012.<sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup> Under current NCAA rules, these schools must have an invitation from an FBS conference in order to move to FBS. Three of them\xe2\x80\x94<a href="/wiki/Appalachian_State_Mountaineers_football" title="Appalachian State Mountaineers football">Appalachian State</a>, <a href="/wiki/Georgia_Southern_Eagles_football" title="Georgia Southern Eagles football">Georgia Southern</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Old_Dominion_Monarchs_football" title="Old Dominion Monarchs football">Old Dominion</a>\xe2\x80\x94began FBS transitions in 2013. All had the required FBS conference invitations, with Old Dominion joining Conference USA in 2013, and Appalachian State and Georgia Southern joining the Sun Belt Conference in 2014. The difference in the postseasons in each of the subdivisions grant the FCS an advantage to have the best record in college football history, 17\xe2\x80\x930, while the FBS only allows a 15\xe2\x80\x930 record.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Football_Bowl_Subdivision">Football Bowl Subdivision</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=NCAA_Division_I&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Football Bowl Subdivision">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/NCAA_Division_I_Football_Bowl_Subdivision" title="NCAA Division I Football Bowl Subdivision">NCAA Division I Football Bowl Subdivision</a></div>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/College_football_national_championships_in_NCAA_Division_I_FBS" title="College football national championships in NCAA Division I FBS">College football national championships in NCAA Division I FBS</a> and <a href="/wiki/List_of_NCAA_Division_I_FBS_football_bowl_records" title="List of NCAA Division I FBS football bowl records">List of NCAA Division I FBS football bowl records</a></div>\n<p><b>Division I Football Bowl Subdivision</b> (<b>FBS</b>), formerly known as <b>Division I-A</b>, is the top level of <a href="/wiki/College_football" title="College football">college football</a>. Schools in Division I FBS compete in post-season <a href="/wiki/List_of_college_bowl_games" title="List of college bowl games">bowl games</a>, with the champions of five conferences, along with the highest-ranked champion of the other five conferences, receiving <a href="/wiki/Automatic_Qualifying_conference" class="mw-redirect" title="Automatic Qualifying conference">automatic bids</a> to the <a href="/wiki/College_Football_Playoff#Bowl_selections" title="College Football Playoff">access bowls</a>.\n</p><p>FBS schools are limited to a total of 85 football players receiving financial assistance.<sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup> For competitive reasons, a student receiving partial scholarship counts fully against the total of 85. Nearly all FBS schools that are not on NCAA probation give 85 full scholarships.\n</p><p>As of the most recently completed 2017 season, there are 129 full members of Division I FBS, with the <a href="/wiki/UAB_Blazers_football" title="UAB Blazers football">University of Alabama at Birmingham</a> (UAB) reinstating a football program that it had dropped after the 2014 season. Two schools, <a href="/wiki/Coastal_Carolina_Chanticleers_football" title="Coastal Carolina Chanticleers football">Coastal Carolina University</a> and <a href="/wiki/Liberty_Flames_football" title="Liberty Flames football">Liberty University</a>, are at varying stages of the FBS transition process. Coastal began its transition in <a href="/wiki/2016_Coastal_Carolina_Chanticleers_football_team" title="2016 Coastal Carolina Chanticleers football team">2016</a>, when it joined the <a href="/wiki/Sun_Belt_Conference" title="Sun Belt Conference">Sun Belt Conference</a> for non-football sports; in that season, Coastal was classified as an FCS independent, but was ineligible for the FCS playoffs. Coastal joined Sun Belt football for the 2017 season and was counted as an FBS member for scheduling purposes, but full FBS membership and bowl eligibility will not come until 2018.<sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup> Liberty, currently a member of the <a href="/wiki/Big_South_Conference" title="Big South Conference">Big South Conference</a>, began its transition in <a href="/wiki/2017_Liberty_Flames_football_team" title="2017 Liberty Flames football team">2017</a>; it remains in the Big South that season, but is ineligible for the FCS playoffs. The NCAA granted Liberty a waiver from its normal FCS-to-FBS transition rules requiring that a school have an invitation from an FBS conference to begin the transition, meaning that Liberty will become an FBS independent for scheduling purposes in 2018 and a full FBS member in 2019.<sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Since the <a href="/wiki/2016_NCAA_Division_I_FBS_football_season" title="2016 NCAA Division I FBS football season">2016 season</a>, all FBS conferences have been allowed to conduct a championship game that does not count against the limit of 12 regular-season contests. Under the current rules, such a game can be held either (1) between the winners of each of two divisions, with each team having played a full round-robin schedule within its division, or (2) between the conference\'s top two teams after a full round-robin conference schedule.<sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup> Previously, "exempt" championship games could only be held by between the divisional winners of conferences that had at least 12 football teams and split into divisions.<sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup> The prize is normally a specific bowl game bid for which the conference has a tie-in.\n</p><p>Some conferences have numbers in their names but this often has no relation to the number of member institutions in the conference. The Big Ten Conference did not formally adopt the "Big Ten" name until 1987, but unofficially used that name when it had 10 members from 1917 to 1946, and again from 1949 forward. However, it has continued to use the name even after it expanded to 11 members with the addition of Penn State in 1990, 12 with the addition of Nebraska <a href="/wiki/2010%E2%80%9314_Big_Ten_Conference_realignment#Nebraska" title="2010\xe2\x80\x9314 Big Ten Conference realignment">in 2011</a>, and 14 with the arrival of Maryland and Rutgers <a href="/wiki/2010%E2%80%9314_Big_Ten_Conference_realignment#Maryland" title="2010\xe2\x80\x9314 Big Ten Conference realignment">in 2014</a>. The Big 12 Conference was established in 1996 with 12 members, but continues to use that name even after a <a href="/wiki/2010%E2%80%9313_Big_12_Conference_realignment" title="2010\xe2\x80\x9313 Big 12 Conference realignment">number of departures and a few replacements</a> left the conference with 10 members. On the other hand, the Pac-12 Conference has used names (official or unofficial) that have reflected the number of members since its current charter was established in 1959. The conference unofficially used "Big Five" (1959\xe2\x80\x9362), "Big Six" (1962\xe2\x80\x9364), and "Pacific-8" (1964\xe2\x80\x9368) before officially adopting the "Pacific-8" name. The name duly changed to "Pacific-10" in 1978 with the addition of Arizona and Arizona State, and "Pac-12" (instead of "Pacific-12") in 2011 when <a href="/wiki/2010%E2%80%932014_NCAA_conference_realignment#Pac-10" title="2010\xe2\x80\x932014 NCAA conference realignment">Colorado and Utah joined</a>. Conferences also tend to ignore their regional names when adding new schools. For example, the Pac-8/10/12 retained its "Pacific" moniker even though its four newest members (Arizona, Arizona State, Colorado, Utah) are located in the inland West, and the <a href="/wiki/Big_East_Conference_(1979%E2%80%932013)" title="Big East Conference (1979\xe2\x80\x932013)">original Big East</a> kept its name even after adding schools (either in all sports or for football only) located in areas traditionally considered to be in the Midwest (Cincinnati, DePaul, Marquette, Notre Dame), Upper South (Louisville, Memphis) and Southwest (Houston, SMU). The non-football conference that <a href="/wiki/Big_East_Conference" title="Big East Conference">assumed the Big East name</a> when the original Big East <a href="/wiki/2010%E2%80%9313_Big_East_Conference_realignment#Catholic_7" title="2010\xe2\x80\x9313 Big East Conference realignment">split in 2013</a> is another example of this phenomenon, as half of its 10 inaugural schools (Butler, Creighton, DePaul, Marquette, Xavier) are traditionally regarded as being Midwestern.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Conferences_2">Conferences</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=NCAA_Division_I&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Conferences">edit</a><span
